4.5    Provincial and Municipal Institutions. HPG is headed by a govemor assisted by
seven vice-govemors, each of whom  oversees several commissions and/or departments.
Various provincial commissions, departments and bureaus provide supervision and



- 33 -
guidance to the municipalities on matters relating to infrastructure, planning, development
and operation, including environmental protection works. Organization charts of the HPG
and the interrelationship of the project implementing agencies with the provincial
government are given in Annex 1O.
4.6    The organization of the municipal governments of Huangshi, Wuhan, Xiangfan and
Yichang is similar. Municipal affairs are overseen by the municipal economic and planning
commissions, and municipal urban and rural construction commissions.  The latter
commissions supervise a number of bureaus which are responsible for the physical
planning, design, construction, operation and maintenance of urban infrastructure, and for
environmental protection. In addition, several bureaus concerned with urban infrastructure
report directly to the office of the mayor. These include the Finance Bureau, the Water
Conservancy Bureau, the Bureau of Public Health and Public Security Bureau.
4.7    As part of China's and Hubei's policy of reforms in the public utilities sector, the
participating municipalities have established self-accounting, and self-funding state-owned
entities to implement the wastewater components of the project. Prior to negotiations, the
Bank/lIDA received (a) conformed copies of the charters, and (b) business licenses of these
agencies. The wastes management component is to be implemented, operated and
maintained by the municipalities' Sanitation Departments/Divisions.
4.8    Wastewater. Until the creation of wastewater entities, wastewater collection,
treatment and disposal were generally provided by the sewerage sections of municipal
facilities management divisions (MFMDs) of the municipal governments. These divisions
reported to the municipal urban construction bureaus (UCBs), which, in turn, came under
the authority of the municipal construction commissions. MFMDs are also responsible for
the operation and maintenance of roads, bridges, parks and street lighting. The sewerage
sections did not operate on a financially autonomous basis, transferring, instead, all tariff
revenue collections to the UCB. Funds were consolidated at the UCB level and allocated to
the various sections for operation, maintenance and capital works, on the basis of budgets
prepared and submitted by the sections. The wastewater entities created for the project will
account for their own capital and operating costs, and existing wastewater assets are being
transferred to the entities' books. While the new entities are still required to remit their
revenues to their respective municipal governments, such funds are earmarked to finance
the operation and maintenance of the sewerage facilities, and service debt. The remitted
revenues, therefore, are returned in their entirety to the entities. While less desired than full
autonomy, water companies function efficiently under such a svstem. Staff from the
existing sewerage sections in the three project cities were transferred to the newly-formned
wastewater entities and have the skills needed to maintain and operate those entities, %% ith
training provided under the project.
4.9    Wastes Management. Domestic and municipal solid waste and nightsoil collection
and disposal are the responsibility of the municipal sanitation departments. Domestic waste
and nightsoil collections are usually performed by subdistrict teams who collect the waste
from the residential units and transfer it, usually by handcart, to transfer stations around the



- 34 -
cities. From there it is collected by the sanitation departments and disposed of at landfills or
dumping grounds, or, in the case of nightsoil, to storage tanks, into the sewerage system, or
to farmers. Neighborhood committees collect fees to offset collection costs.  Other
domestic costs are covered by a flat tax per employee and through general government
revenues. Industrial (including hazardous/toxic) and commercial wastes are disposed of by
the enterprises themselves, or by the sanitation departments, against payment of fees or
service charges.  Infectious hospital waste is normally incinerated by the hospitals
themselves; the domestic hospital waste is disposed of in the same manner as other
domestic waste. Recycling is practiced widely in all the project cities. The recyclables are
mainly ferrous and nonferrous metals, rubber, plastic, glass, paper and textiles. The
recyclables are usually brought to municipal-run collection points by the waste producers
themselves or by informal-sector operators. The latter collect material from producers,
from collection points, transfer stations or the disposal sites. Some of the recyclables
collected by the informal sector are sold directly to enterprises.
4.10  Industrial Pollution Control Line-of-Credit. The People's Construction Bank of
China, Hubei Branch, acting as agent for Hubei Provincial Finance Bureau, will be
responsible for appraising specific subprojects to be financed under the line of credit.
PCBC was appraised by the Bank/IDA and found to have the staff and administrative
capacity to undertake satisfactorily the agent role. A list of seven first-priority, and three
second-priority subprojects were selected by the Hubei Provincial Planning Commission,
(HPPC), working with HEPB and other regulatory agencies. Two of these subprojects
(Ezhou Iron & Steel and Yichang Cement) have been appraised jointly by PCBC and the
Bank/IDA to assess the appraisal process and criteria to be used.
4.11  Preparation work on the remaining subprojects will be carried out by the individual
enterprises. PCBC will conduct the financial and technical appraisal for the prepared
subprojects.   Subproject appraisal documentation will also include environmental
assessments and resettlement action plans prepared in conformity with Bank guidelines.
PCBC will report appraisal findings to HFB, which will confirm final approval of the
subproject with HPPC. The appraisal reports for these subprojects will then be sent to the
Bank/IDA by HPPC, through HUEPO, for approval before a subloan is approved. In
addition, PCBC will administer the disbursement and collection of funds for the
subprojects, review the contract packages, and determine the methods of procurement
required. In this capacity, PCBC will bear no financial risks. Assurances were obtained
dcuring negotiations that procedures and criteria for subproject appraisal, satisfactory to
the BanklIDA, would be followed. The project files contain the appraisal criteria which
have been agreed upon with HP. The signing of a financial agency agreement, satisfactory
to the Bank/IDA, between HP and a bank, including the relevant procedures and for the
provision of the necessary services, would he a condition of disbursement for this
subcomponent (para. 3.22).
4.12   Environmental Pollution Control Fund. The Hubei Environmental Pollution
Control Fund will be operated as a part of HEPB, although with segregated accounting and
special operating procedures.  The management and operation of the HEPCF are



- 35 -
summarized in Annex 12. The purpose of the HEPCF will be to provide funds for
environmental pollution control, targeted to small-scale enterprises.  Project selection
criteria have been proposed and generally follow that of similar funds in operation
elsewhere in China. Projects will be selected according to cost effectiveness and finaic;ia,
viability, with the first three appraisals and all appraisals for proposed subloans of over
$300,000 receiving ex-ante review by the Bank and the remainder subject to ex-post review.
Appraisal criteria, both technical and financial, and environmental and resettlement
requirements parallel those used in the Industrial Pollution Control Line-of-Credit described
in the previous paragraph. The principal funding sources for HEPCF will be fees levied on
pollution discharges (para. 3. 10), the proceeds of the Loan/Credit and recycling of loan and
interest payments. The approval by the Bank/IDA of an operational manual governing
HEPCF subloan processing and approval, and the execution of an acceptable financial
agency agreement between HP and a bank for subloan processing, would he conditions of
disbursement (para. 3.22).
4.13 Water Quality Monitoring and Management: Hubei Environmental
Monitoring Center (HEMC). HEMC, which was established in 1975, reports to the
Hubei Environmental Protection Bureau (HEPB). HEMC does not carry out routine
monitoring, but rather is responsible for supervising the work of municipal (Level 3) and
county (Level 4) monitoring centers in Hubei Province. These centers collect and analyze
the regular environmental monitoring samples from river cross-sections and lake sampling
points. The results are sent to HEMC where they are collated and analyzed to provide
provincial environmental quality reports. HEMC's major functions are: (a) technical
direction and training of Level 3 and 4 monitoring centers; (b) establishment and
coordination of the provincial environmental monitoring network; (c) environmental
pollution investigations; (d) data management and analyses; (e) development of
environmental quality standards; (f) development and validation of new test protocols;
(g) certification of Level 3 monitoring centers; (h) major environmental impact
assessments; (i) complex analyses which cannot be performed by municipal and COullt)
centers; and (j) research and development.
4.14  HEMC is headed by a director and has a staff of about 120, including some 80 well-
qualified technical staff. Its organizational structure includes 18 departments, of which 14
are technical and the balance administrative.
4.15 Water Quality Monitoring and Management: Wuhan Environmental
Monitoring Center (WEMC). WEMC, which was established in 1979 as a Level 3
(municipal) monitoring center, is one of the main divisions of the Wuhan Environmental
Protection Bureau, and reports to that Bureau. WEMC is responsible for monitoring
(a) water quality, (b) air quality, and (c) solid wastes. In addition to carrying out its own
analyses, WEMC oversees and compiles data from 13 Level 4 monitoring centers within
Wuhan Municipality. It produces reports for the national and provincial monitoring centers
which document industrial wastewater, surface water quality and statistical aialyses of
sampling results.  WEMC has also been given responsibility for a WorlJ  I lealtli



- 36 -
Organization Global Environmental Monitoring Station on the Yangtze River, upstream of
the Han River.
4.16   WEMC is headed by a director and has a staff of 140, including some 135 well-
qualified  technical staff.  Its organizational structure includes  13 departments,
encompassing the following four analytical functions: (a) water quality monitoring; (b) air
quality monitoring; (c) biotic monitoring; and (d) physical testing.
4.17  Institutional Development and Training. As the wastewater entities have been
created only recently and the solid waste bureaus lack financial management tools, the
project institutional development and training component has been designed specifically to
address the accounting, management information, financial management, utility pricing,
technical requirements and training needs of the entities, in particular, through activities
supported by Packages A and B (see Annex 2). Prior to negotiations HPG provided a
signed contract, acceptable to the Bank/IDA, for the provision of construction management
and operational training consulting services under Package B (para. 3.17). HPG expects to
sign a contract for the provision of financial consulting services under Package A by
effectiveness of the Loan and Credit.
C. STATUS OF ENGINEERING
4.18  Xiangfan identified an alternative landfill site late in the project preparation cycle.
To test this alternative, the Xiangfan Municipal Government intends to complete, not
later  than  December  31,  1996, (a) a  feasibility  study;  (b) detailed  design;
(c) environmental assessment; and (d) land acquisition and resettlement plan (para. 3.22).
4.19   Project preparation and design work has been undertaken jointly by international
and local consultants and design bureaus, and is well advanced. Work on final design
and draft bidding document for (a) the landfill operating equipment and solid waste
vehicles for Wuhan, Yichang and Huangshi; (b) Wuhan: Shahu/Erlangmiao/Longwangzui
civil works and equipment supply and installation; (c) Xiangfan: Fancheng interceptors
civil works; and (d) Yichang: Huangjiawan sanitary landfill, representing approximately
20 percent of municipal component investments was completed in June 1995. The
remaining final design and bidding document preparation work will continue during the
period prior to effectiveness.



- 37 -
5. FINANCE
A. FINANCIAL MANAGEMENT
5.1   Since 1978, Chinese economic reforms have evolved gradually toward an
increasingly decentralized financial system. Most industrial production and prices have
become free of government control, and enterprises have to purchase most of their inputs
at market prices. Enterprise reform has become a high priority of the Government. In
this connection, the guiding principles of the recently promulgated "regulations on
transforming the management mechanisms of state-owned industrial enterprises" are to
separate government administration from enterprise management, increase the autonomy
and accountability of state-owned enterprises, and increase competition and market
orientation. The reformn efforts to improve economic policies and enterprise efficiencies
are essential for sustaining both economic development and environmental protection in
the longer run.
5.2   In order for the enterprises to assume their increased responsibilities efficiently and
effectively, strengthening their financial management is essential. Toward this end, the
proposed project would support the application of modem management techniques and
human resource development for the utility companies.
B. ACCOUNTING
5.3   Different accounting systems are used by enterprises (autonomous units),
construction units (which execute investment projects) and local government
departments, in accordance with regulations prescribed by the Ministry of Finance.
5.4   Enterprise Accounting. All the state-owned enterprises in China follow a unified
enterprise accounting system. The accounting framework was developed in the context of a
highly centralized planned economy. With a view to complementing the ongoing efforts to
deepen reforms in the financial sector and enterprise management, the accounting system in
China needed modernization to permit satisfactory financial management in an increasingly
decentralized and market-oriented environment. As a first step, the general principles of
enterprise (the term to include public utilities such as water, sewerage, and district heating
companies) accounting were revised by the Ministry of Finance from July 1, 1993 to be
more consistent with internationally generally accepted accounting practice. As part of the
reforms, enterprises are required to produce five reports for submission to (Governrmeii:
balance sheet, income statement, source and application of funds, profit distribution, and
details of main business. The first two reports are required monthly, the remainder on an



- 38 -
annual basis.  All enterprise implementing agencies included in the project will be
following the new accounting and reporting formats.
5.5    Government Accounting. The regular government department accounts reflect
expenditures incurred against budget allocations by HPG or their municipal government.
Accounting is handled in a decentralized manner through transfers to subunits from which
actual expenditures are made and recorded on a cash basis. Accounts are kept at all levels,
and each subunit reflects in its accounts the budget allocation it receives from the unit
above.
5.6   Project Accounts. Project accounts would be maintained by HUEPO and the
environmental sanitation divisions of Huangshi, Wuhan, Xiangfan and Yichang in
accordance with the State Construction Unit Accounting System. This system prescribes
generally accepted accounting practices, including double entry bookkeeping, subsidiary
ledger accounting, and rules for capitalizing and expensing various items of expenditure. It
also sets out detailed accounting definitions for capital works, cash control, receivables and
payables. In addition, it identifies a number of basic monthly, quarterly and annual
reporting fornats, including funds flow statements and balance sheets. The construction
accounting system is comprehensive, and is adequate for the proposed project. Project-
related expenditures incurred by enterprises (e.g., the wastewater entities and the industries
to be included in the industrial pollution control component), would be reflected within
their respective enterprise accounting systems.
C. AUDITS
5.7    As with other IBRD-financed projects in China, the Foreign Investment Audit
Bureau of the State Audit Administration (SAA), established in 1983, would have overall
responsibility for auditing accounts concerning the project. The actual auditing work will
be conducted by the Hubei Provincial Audit Department under SAA's supervision. This
arrangement is satisfactory. During negotiations, assurances were obtained that the
following annual audits would be submitted to the BanklIDA within six months after the end
of the financial year: (a) audit of the project accounts maintained by HUEPO and the
environmental sanitation divisions of Huangshi, Wuhan, Xiangfan and Yichang; (h) audit of
the Special Account; (c) audit of Statements of Expenditure (SOEs); (d) audits of the
financial statements of the wastewater entities of Huangshi, Wuhan and Xiangfan, and
(e) audit of HEPCF. The Bank/lIDA would expect to receive the first such audit reports by
June 30, 1997, covering project implementation activities commencing in fiscal year 1996.
D. PROVINCIAL FINANCE
5.8   The Hubei Provincial Government has two types of finance, on-budgetary and off-
budgetary. On-budgetary revenues are provided through taxes, the base and rate of which
are set by the Central Government. The central government directly controls the single
largest tax source-the value-added tax-receipts from which are shared with local
government. A variety of other taxes are collected and used directly by local government.
Most off-budgetary revenues consist of special fees paid by enterprises, and fees and



- 39 -
charges relating to construction activities. These are used for capital expenditures. On-
budgetary and off-budgetary accounts are not consolidated. While information on off-
budgetary revenues and expenditures is confidential, and was not, therefore, provided to the
Bank/IDA, they are said to be at least as large as the on-budgetary data.
5.9   HP will recover the debt associated with the project as it is being passed on to the
municipalities and industrial enterprises. HPG will monitor municipal finances to assure
that the municipalities and their respective agencies meet their debt service obligations.
Should the municipalities and their subborrowers be unable to fulfill their obligations, HP
would have no difficulty in covering debt service through its own resources, as the debt
service represented by the project is small compared to total provincial budgetary
expenditures. Provincial fiscal budgetary revenues and expenditures totaled about Y 13.1
billion ($1.6 billion) and Y 10.9 billion ($1.3 billion), respectively, in 1994. Between
1991 and 1994, revenues and expenditures increased on average by about 11 and
3 percent a year, respectively. Revenues are derived mainly from taxes on industry and
commerce, which have been remitted by the municipalities. Revenues and expenditures
are projected to grow at about 5 percent a year in real terms during the project period.
E. MUNICIPAL FINANCE
5.10 HP will, through the municipalities of Huangshi, Wuhan, Xiangfan and Yichang,
provide all funding not met by (a) the proceeds of the Bank Loan and IDA Credit, and
(b) funds generated  internally  by  the  implementing  agencies.   During  project
implementation, the financial projections show that these requirements would be of the
order of Y 109 million for Huangshi, Y 923 million for Wuhan, Y 338 million for
Xiangfan, and Y 62 million for Yichang. The municipalities, like HPG, have two sources
of finance, on-budget and off-budget. The off-budget details are confidential, and were not,
therefore, provided to the Bank/IDA. The municipal funding requirements would come
from many more sources than municipal on-budget revenue. The Hubei Finance Bureau
(HFB) has independently assessed the municipalities' abilitv to generate the required
counterpart funds from assured sources.
5.11   Average annual project-related expenditures comprise between about I to 4 percent
of the municipalities' annual total on-budget expenditures, and between 20 and 40 percent
of their annual capital expenditures, except for Xiangfan where the planned expenditures
were substantially curtailed due to counterpart funding constraints, but still temporarily
represent about a twofold increase in capital expenditures. During negotiations, assurancc..
were obtained from HPG that it would provide, directly or through the municipalitie.s, the
necessary counterpartfunds for the execution of the project.
F. WASTEWATER AGENCIES
5.12  As the wastewater agencies, which evolved out of the cities' departmental
wastewater operations, were established only in the latter phases of the project
preparation cycle, no assessment of their past performance was possible. Based on the
limited data available, it was determined that the Huangshi and Xiangfan departmental



- 40 -
wastewater operations were able to meet their operating expenses, excluding
depreciation, in 1994. As is the practice in departmental operations, no provision for
depreciation was made. Had depreciation charges been included, their operations would
have run at a loss. In Huangshi, the revenue from wastewater operations in 1994 was
calculated to be Y 3.5 million, based on 1993 drainage fee collections of Y 1.9 million
plus industrial discharge fee collections of Y 1.2 million, adjusted to reflect some
inflation. In Xiangfan, no operating revenues were assumed. Instead, an amount of Y 4.7
million was assumed as nonoperating revenue, representing discharge fees paid to
Xiangfan EPB. As no departmental revenue data were made available from Wuhan, the
financial projections were developed from the net historical value of investments made in
the wastewater sector. The financial objective set by HPG for the newly-established
wastewater companies is to achieve full cost recovery, and, if possible, generate some
funds for future capital investment.

G. WASTES MANAGEMENT
5.18   Solid waste and nightsoil management services are general municipal services
which have traditionally been funded from the general revenues of local governments.
However, where individual users of the services have been identified, local governments
have, as a policy matter, charged such users for the services provided. Thus, commercial
and industrial entities which haul their refuse to landfill for disposal, or contract with
their respective sanitation departments to do so, have been charged a disposal fee.
5.19   The 1993 recurrent cost recovery ratios for solid waste management in the project
cities were as follows: Huangshi-43 percent; Wuhan-4 1 percent; Xiangfan-26 percent;
and Yichang -35 percent. Huangshi and Wuhan have shown a steadily improving
performance over the period 1990-93; Xiangfan's performance over the same period has
been static.  Yichang commenced charging for its service only in 1992 when its cost
recovery ratio was 40 percent. The 1993 cost recovery ratios for nightsoil among the cities
varied from 8 percent to 20 percent.
5.20   All four project cities have considered increasing their fees and charges, and in two
of the cities the appropriate authorities have already approved higher charges for some parts
of the services. The cost recovery objective for solid waste and nightsoil services for all
four cities is to recover the full unit cost of collection and disposal where user identification
and billing is economically feasible. The balance of the cost for providing these services
would be met from the general revenues of the municipality.



- 44 -
5.21   During the implementation phase of this component the cities have indicated that
they intend to apply a portion of the proceeds of their respective solid waste and nightsoil
user charges and fees towards meeting their counterpart funding requirements. During
negotiations, assurances were obtained from HPG that it would ensure that Huangshi,
Wuhan, Xiangfan, and Yichang Municipalities would each: (a) not later than April 1,
1997, complete a study of the fees and user charges needed for the recovery of the full
costs of providing solid waste collection and disposal services to industrial and
commercial users; the report of that study shall include a detailed, time-bound action
plan, acceptable to the Bank, enabling such recovery as of July 1, 1997; and
(b) thereafter implement the said action plan taking into account the Bank/IDA's
comments thereon.
5.22   As these services have been decentralized to the district and subdistrict levels, and
because the accounting systems, particularly at the lower levels, are weak, accurate costs of
providing these services have been difficult to determine. The project, therefore, includes
technical assistance and training to help develop appropriate accounting, including unit cost
accounting, and management information systems for determining accurate unit costs of the
services at the various stages, e.g., collection, transportation, treatment (in the case of
nightsoil), and final disposal. With such information, the cities would then be in a better
position tu (a) assess the efficiency and cost effectiveness of the services; (b) price their
services to recover the full economic cost where user identification and billing is
economically feasible; and (c) judge whether the services, or parts thereof, can be further
commercialized or privatized.
I}. INDUSTRIAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL POLLUTION CONTROL SUBPROJECTS
5.23   As some of the priority pollution control subprojects are, by nature, not
financially productive, the financial appraisal would focus on the long-term financial
health of the enterprises.  The key considerations in this assessment include the
enterprise's development goals and strategy, product mix and market analysis, investment
requirements and related financing arrangements, and projected financial performance in
terms of profitability, liquidity and debt servicing capabilities.  The two industrial
pollution control subprojects that Bank/IDA staff have already appraised meet the above
financial criteria and therefore are considered financially viable.  With a view to
promoting prudent financial management, the agreed subproject selection criteria call for
the entity to maintain a debt service coverage ratio of not less than 1.4 times after the
proposed subproject has reached full production capacity.



- 45 -
6. ECONOMIC, SOCIAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL
CONSIDERATIONS
A. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
6.1   As described in Chapter 2, the project derives from the CAS in its support of
provincial efforts to substantially reduce pollution within the province, especially within
densely populated urban areas, by improving environmental regulation and providing new
models for the efficient organization and financing of urban environmental services.
Specifically, the project would support:
(a)   the more aggressive use of user fees to shift the burden of pollution
abatement to the polluter and induce waste minimization, and new
organizational forms for wastewater providers, to allow improved
management tools and to help minimize the cost of abatement;
(b)   the provincial and municipal EPBs in their efforts to implement more
effective regulatory instruments;
(c)   expansion of sewerage services, hence a reduction in water pollution, within
the boundaries of Wuhan, Xiangfan, and Huangshi, thus protecting the
urban environment and the health of their people;
(d)   the first municipal solid waste landfills meeting national standards in the
cities of Xiangfan and Yichang, and major capacity expansion in Huangshi
and Wuhan, with consequent protection of local land and water resources
and flaring of landfill methane to reduce greenhouse gas emissions; and
(e)   a line of credit to allow abatement of important industrial point sources of
pollution affecting large urban populations and a pollution control revolving
fund to provide a model for such funds.
B. ECONoMic ANALYSIS
6.2   Current methodologies do not permit high-confidence benefit estimates for the
major municipal investments in wastewater and solid waste collection and treatment
proposed under this project. Although some benefits, such as restoring Wuhan's East
Lake as a source of potable water, can be readily quantified, measurement becomes
exceedingly problematic for the complex of aesthetic, health, agricultural, and other
impacts that often reach far beyond the political boundaries of the polluter. For this



- 46 -
reason, project formulation did not utilize traditional benefit-cost analysis as the basis for
allocating resources.
6.3    During project identification, it became clear that Hubei and its cities needed
substantial change in policy and institutional structures along the lines described in the
sector work reported in Chapter 1 (China: Urban Environmental Service Management).
Policy problems included urban environmental services priced too low to discourage
waste generation or provide the financing needed to build, maintain, and operate services
such as wastewater collection and treatment. Government bureaus providing the services
lacked the financial tools to develop effective tariff strategies or the operating autonomy
needed to encourage managerial innovation. Gaps in environmental regulation were also
apparent. Therefore, investment choices were made with a view toward maximizing the
impact of policy and institutional innovation within the province. Specific investment
choices within sectors and cities responded to long-run municipal government strategies
to bring ambient environmental quality or service standards up to national guidelines and
were evaluated on a least-cost option basis. The analysis is discussed in more detail in
Annex 14 and the detailed calculations can be found in the project files. Highlights of the
analysis are given below.
6.4    City and Sector Choices. In urban environmental service provision, cities were
included in the project based on the size of the population potentially benefiting from
improved services and considering their value as models to other provincial cities. After
reviewing potential investments in some nine provincial cities, the four largest cities were
selected for further project development.  Each of those cities had already prepared
environmental improvement plans that outlined priority service improvements, both in
terms of the type of service and areas of the city needing service. Work with Bank missions
confirmed the choice to extend sewerage systems and build or substantially expand sanitary
landfills as the next step in environmental protection.
6.5    Credit market constraints (described much more fully in the China Department's
1995 Capital Markets Sector Study (No. 14501-CHA)) leave unexploited a large number of
abatement opportunities in economically and financially viable enterprises that currently
have a high pollution impact on large populations. Hubei Province therefore sought to use
part of the loan to fund such investments. The Province agreed with the Bank on a selection
process to identify the highest-impact polluters and provide them access to a line-of-credit
to finance abatement interventions.
6.6    Given that urban environmental problems could be successfully addressed only by a
combination of urban service and industrial pollution control measures, and that the high
visibility of Bank-financed operations was thought important to providing model
approaches to outstanding problems, HPG and the Bank/IDA agreed that both types of
activities should be incorporated into the loan and credit. The allocation of the loan and
credit between the two types of activities, approximately two thirds to municipal services
and one third to industrial and environmental pollution control, resulted from a shared
judgment about the balance that would produce maximum impact within the province.



- 47 -
6.7    Wastewater.  Wastewater collection and treatment programs are discussed in
detail in Annex 14. In no city were counterpart funds sufficient to finance completion of
wastewater collection systems. Therefore, the engineering and economic analysis sought
to identify the largest wastewater loads that could be picked up with availablc funds-a
variation on the least-cost approach. For Xiangfan, whose water pollution affects urban
tributaries to the Han River, and that river itself, only one of the two major urban districts
could be sewered. Of the two, Fancheng district could be handled at lower cost and was
therefore selected (Map 27175). The interceptor sewer will pick up most discharges to
the Xiaoqinghe and Qilihe Rivers, the largest within Fancheng, with a resulting quality
increase from below usable standard to Class III, which permits agricultural and
industrial use.2 The wastewater will discharge through a preliminary treatment plant to
the Han River, where a net improvement in water quality must await later primary and
secondary treatment. For Huangshi, on the Yangtze (Map 27175), the largest problem is
Cihu lake, major parts of which have deteriorated from Class III to worse than Class V in
a decade. Of the planned northern and southern trunk sewers around Cihu, the southern
would serve the largest sources of Cihu pollution and 120,000 people. This investment
alone is estimated to bring the lake back to Class III standard. Modeling showed that the
additional discharges to the Yangtze could be safely assimilated. Wuhan had lost the use
of 10 percent of municipal water supply previously extracted from East Lake, which has
fallen to Class IV in many areas. The cost of that loss had been an average of Y 20
million per year in replacement supply from the Yangtze. The chosen intervention of
diversion plus primary treatment will cost Y 640 million and restore East Lake quality to
Class IlI.
6.8    Solid Waste. Solid waste landfills are also discussed in detail in Annex 14. Here,
initial choices were among potential solid waste disposal strategies that included recycling,
composting, incineration, and landfilling. Recycling plays an active role in each of the
cities, captunrng most of the 5 percent of the municipal waste stream that can be profitably
recycled. The industry is highly competitive, with little scope for additional useful
municipal intervention. Composting, to recycle organic materials, has been tested with
scant success in Wuhan and other Chinese cities. Costs have been high and markets have
not supported product sales. A major problem for composting and incineration is that over
half of the waste stream is inorganic, with coal ash the dominant inorganic component.
Analysis in other cities with similar waste streams has shown incineration to be
substantially more costly than landfill. Based on that work, each of the four cities has
elected to use landfilling to handle their municipal solid wastes.
6.9    The cities have accepted that such landfills must comply with national standards, so
made no attempt to compare the cost of adding leachate and gas collection with the damage
that would ensue from their absence. Given the long run risks inherent in unprotected
2 The water quality classification system: Classes I and II: high quality suitable for drinking water
source; Class Ill: usable for drinking water after treatment; Class IV: boating, fishing, industrial water
supply; Class V: irrigation and industrial cooling.



- 48 -
landfills, the Bank accepted without additional impact evaluation the municipal judgment
on the need to provide leachate and gas collection and treatment. The least cost site and
design had, as one of the main decision criteria, minimization of the joint cost of transport
and land acquisition. As land acquisition cost correlates very strongly with resettlement
impact, this criteria also tends to minimize resettlement. For Wuhan, the availability of
suitable land adjoining an existing landfill determined the site choice.  Xiangfan is
continuing analysis of two alternative sites, while Yichang and Huangshi have both
confirmed sites among their alternatives.
6.10  Industrial Pollution Control. The industrial pollution control component has two
subcomponents, one a line of credit for major investments and the other a pollution control
fund targeting small investments and small-scale enterprises.  Eligibility criteria were
discussed in para. 2.9 and, second to the environmental impact ranking, require a
financially and economically viable enterprise. Once that is demonstrated, proposed
abatement measures would be appraised for technical feasibility and economic least cost.
Given the difficulty of valuing environmental externalities, an economic rate of return
would not normnally be calculated. This approach recognizes that the most efficient
pollution abatement investments will not necessarily produce a positive financial or easily
valued economic return and therefore focuses on the fundamental soundness of the
enterprise. The firm ranked most damaging among the viable firms was Ezhou Iron and
Steel Company (Egang). The firm's proposed abatement projects were jointly appraised by
Bank and PCBC staff to test the domestic appraisal process. Other line-of-credit applicants
must pass similar appraisals, all of which will be subject to Bank prior review.
6.11   The successful appraisal (see Annex 14, paras. 26-32 for fuller treatment) covered
two subcomponents at the enterprise, one for air pollution abatement and the other
wastewater treatment. The key appraisal finding showed the firm to be financially
healthy and economically viable. The firm benefited from progressive price liberalization
after 1992, and in 1994 had net profits of Y 220 million on sales of Y 1,520 million and
low debt compared to the industry average. The latter finding was particularly important
given the rapid integration of China's steel industry into the international market
economy. In terms of proposed interventions, the first Egang subcomponent will replace
a 40-year old electric arc furnace that is responsible for 23 percent of the TSP
concentrations within an urban area containing some 200,000 people. The proposed
investment is in a new furnace, incorporating up-to-date air pollution control, which will
produce virtually no particulates or oxides of nitrogen (NOJ) and will improve air quality
for affected people from Class III to Class II. The smallest economically viable furnaces
now have a capacity of about 300,000 tons/year, some 20 percent higher than the current
furnace. This capacity increase, coupled with input efficiency gains and the higher
quality output of modern furnaces, will give that subcomponent a positive financial and
economic return before accounting for the environmental benefits. The investment itself
will yield an estimated 12 percent financial and 23 percent economic rate of return, where
the economic rate of return incorporates none of the air quality gains. Most of the gap
between the financial and economic rates of return is due to the fact that Egang steel is



- 49 -
priced 20 to 30 percent below CIF imported prices for equivalent products. That gap
leaves substantial room to absorb price pressures should the world steel market soften.
6.12 The analysis also looked at the sensitivity of the economic rate of returns to
various changes. The four factors judged to have the greatest impact on the economic
rate of return were changes in the economic price of steel, an increase in input costs
relative to sales prices, higher-than-anticipated capital costs, and lower-than-anticipated
efficiency levels (Annex 14, para. 29). To drop the ERR to 12 percent, a combination of
factors including a 20 percent drop in sales price (to over 40 percent below intemational
market levels), a 10 percent increase in capital costs, and a 10 percent "learning curve"
drop in efficiency would have to occur simultaneously. Note that this ERR does not
consider environmental benefits, hence understates true returns. This or similar adverse
combinations are judged highly unlikely given Egang's operating experience and recent
market history in the key input and output markets.
6.13 The high and robust economic returns to the proposed air pollution control
investment are an example of a "win-win" solution to an environmental problem.
However, Egang also includes an example of damaging wastewater discharges that are
difficult to value, and the main available intervention increases operating costs with no
corresponding increase in a marketable product. Egang discharges some 17 tons a year of
phenol, 11.8 tons of cyanide, and 462 tons of oil, as well as large amounts of solids and
chemical oxygen demand (COD) into local watercourses and then the Yangtze. The
phenol and cyanide contribute over 90 percent of all such discharges in Ezhou city and
the oil 63 percent, with the result that the major urban waterways fall to category IV for
phenol and oil, and II for cyanide.
6.14  To reduce these discharges, Egang will invest an estimated Y 130 million and
substantially revamp their wastewater handling system. All discharges currently entering
the Yanglan Lake or Xingang River will be intercepted, treated, and reused, ending
Egang's contribution to their pollution. Yanglan Lake is fished and used as an industrial
water supply source. The Xingang River provides potable water for villagers and a
source of industrial water supply. No epidemiological work has been done on the impact
of Egang discharges through fish consumption or domestic water supply, so damage
estimates were not possible. The most serious discharges, from the coking plant, will
continue to discharge directly to the Yangtze. However, a new coking wastewater
treatment plant financed by the project will radically reduce pollutants in that discharge,
with, for example, phenol falling from 1.53 tons/year to 0.45 tons/year and oil from
32.4 tons/year to 6.5 tons/year. The net effect of the investment will be to bring the
Xingang River and Yanglan Lake to Class II in these pollutants, allowing safe use of the
water.
C. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT
6.15   The project would contribute significantly to improving Hubei's environment, in
ways detailed in the body of this report and its annexes. Potential adverse impacts from



- 50 -
the sanitary landfill and industrial pollution control components require an "A" category
in terms of the World Bank's Operational Directive on Environmental Assessment (EA).
Accordingly, a full EA has been completed, reviewed by the Bank and approved by
NEPA. The full report can be found in the project files. Annex 7 details the EA findings
and reflects the findings of the appraisal mission. The resettlement impact of the project
will be managed through a resettlement action plan that can be found in the project files
and is summarized in Annex 6.
D. AFFORDABILITY AND POVERTY ASSESSMENT
6.16   The affordability issue arises only with the wastewater components of the
proposed project.  The industrial pollution interventions will proceed only if the
enterprise can demonstrate that it will remain financially sound after the investment, and
solid waste charges will rise only for industrial and commercial users of the new landfills.
Affordability of the new wastewater systems was tested against current consumption
patterns and average income of the poorest 10 percent of the urban population in the three
cities. The proposed first-year tariff would consume an average of 0.25 percent of the
income of the poorest 10 percent, based on 80 percent of water used. Indeed, because the
poor are less likely than other households to have water piped to the house (using
standpipes instead, for which no fees are currently charged), even the 0.25 percent figure
represents an upper bound.
6.17   The affordability of the higher tariffs anticipated in later years will clearly depend
on changes in real income among the urban poor over time. In real termns, the tariffs will
increase to as much as twice the anticipated 1996 rate. With no change in real income
and no consumption effect, the average wastewater bill would increase to 0.50 percent of
average income for the poorest 10 percent of households. However, over the past decade,
the real incomes of the urban poor have risen in line with overall income increases. If
that pattem, and overall real personal income growth, continues near current rates,
wastewater tariffs as a percentage of personal income would remain virtually unchanged
from the first-year level. In absolute terms, the increase would be fully offset by first
year income gains.
E. PROJECT Risks
6.18   The proposed project faces or mitigates a variety of risks. Those risks are here
grouped under three general headings: implementation and operational risks, regulatory
risk, and risks to long-term sustainability. Each type of risk is discussed in detail in
Annex 14. Those findings will be briefly summarized here.
6.19   Project financing presents a risk due to the need for municipal counterpart
funding. Among the four cities, Xiangfan has committed the highest percentage of its
on-budget resources to the project.  The final investment represents a substantial
reduction on Xiangfan's requested program, in an attempt to minimize the financing risk.
Additional mitigation is provided by the phasing of the Xiangfan solid waste investments



- 51 -
and by independent provincial finance bureau affirmation of Bank findings on the
adequacy of counterpart funds.
6.20  The shift to new organizational forms for wastewater management is critical to
project success. Slow institutional innovation therefore becomes a project risk. All
cities are well along in the process, with Xiangfan having successfully completed the
transformation to an enterprise. In Huangshi, assets have been revalued and that work is
being reviewed by the State Asset Bureau, the last step before formal transfer. Wuhan has
begun revaluing assets and expects to undertake the transfer by the end of 1995, well before
planned effectiveness.  The groundwork is thus laid for introduction of the new
accounting and management information systems needed to efficiently guide the work of
these enterprises. In the long run, efficient operation will also depend on cities honoring
the autonomy of the enterprises in personnel and other management decisions. This
element of risk cannot be divorced from larger efforts within the economy to reform
government relations with the state owned enterprise sector. These issues have recently
received thorough analysis in State Owned Enterprise Reform (currently in yellow cover).
6.21   The industrial and environmental pollution control components create credit risk
born by the provincial government. Under the line of credit, the agent bank, for a fee,
undertakes project appraisal and handles fund administration on behalf of the provincial
goverrnment. An essential element of the project appraisal, which is subject to review and
confirmation by the provincial finance bureau, is analysis of the firm's financial strength
and the risk it faces in its input and output markets. Subloans to be made by HEPCF will
be governed by operating procedures that remain to be approved by the Bank/IDA.
HEPCF will also enter into an agreement with a bank for subloan processing. All
enterprises must meet a 1.4 debt service coverage requirement over the project life,
providing that margin of safety to the government exposure. The loans are being made at
no less than 150 basis points over the Bank variable lending rate, in US dollars, with the
enterprise bearing the foreign exchange risk. With fees to the agent bank amounting to
no more than 50 basis points, the provincial government will capture at least 100 basis
points to cover their costs and risks. In addition, they will enjoy the use of loan and
interest payments in the interval between collection from the enterprise and repayment to
the Ministry of Finance.
6.22  Operational risks arise in the industrial and environmental pollution control
components with the failure (documented in "China: Urban Environmental Services
Management") of many enterprises to operate their pollution abatement facilities. The
analysis addressed the risk that pollution abatement goals would not be met. Based on
the short-listed project designs, this risk is deemed to be very small for the reason that,
although not a requirement for access to the line-of-credit, the bulk of the proposed
investments are in process changes. The production process itself is inherently much
cleaner than the original process, leading to the pollution control gains
6.23   Major policy risks include possible resistance to timely implementation of the new
tariff structure. Failure to implement agreed tariffs would seriously impair the performance



- 52 -
incentives of the utilities and their ability to finance ongoing investment programs. To
mitigate this risk, a tariff increase before negotiation is being required and tariffs will be a
supervision focal point. The latter will be aided by the fact that service providers are
required to present annual accounts showing whether existing tariffs have met their
financial needs and to project tariff levels needed in the following year.
6.24   Significant regulatory risks also arise because the pollution control results
anticipated for the surface waters depend not only on sewerage technologies successfully
constructed and operated by the cities. Many important pollutants must be controlled at
source by the industries generating them. A failure to do so could threaten either the
sewerage infrastructure or the quality of the various lakes and rivers, depending on
enterprise discharge points. The principal means of mitigating this risk is a strong
regulatory regime. The importance of this work was recognized during preparation of the
project and is addressed under the environmental action plan (Annex 11).
6.25   The major risks to long term sustainability are financial and regulatory, and have
been discussed above. Additional risks arise from outside of the cities themselves and
center on polluting inputs to the various water bodies from areas beyond municipal
control. These additional pollutants could overwhelm any benefit that would otherwise
be realized by the planned investments. In the case of the network of waterways within
the cities, this risk is not great. Most of their watersheds are small, pollutants are of local
origin, and their control will be a function of the proper operation of the project.
However, the Han and Yangtze suffer from nonpoint source pollutants, particularly
agricultural, and carry substantial nutrient loads from upstream agriculture as well as
industrial pollution discharges. Satisfactory abatement of those polluting sources will
depend on the actions of a large number of political jurisdictions, most of which would
suffer no immediate harm from continued discharges to these rivers. The growing
appreciation within China of the importance of water pollution abatement offers the best
hope of inducing compliance with the current industrial regulations.  A  similar
appreciation of the risks from agriculture has yet to appear, and must become an element
of river basin quality control.



- 53 -
