ANNEX 14: ECONOMIC, SOCIAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL
CONSIDERATIONS
A. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
1.    The Bank Group highlighted enhancing environmental protection and alleviating
infrastructure bottlenecks as two of the four areas of concentration under the China Country
A\ssistance Strategy (CAS) presented to the Board in June 1995. The Bank Group's close
collaboration witlh the Chinese government covers both the regulatory and service provision
aspects of urban environmental maniagement. The China. Environmental Strategy Paper
sector study led to an Environmental 'Technical Assistance Project that assists NEPA in its
ef'forts to find more efficient regulatory methods, improve staff capabilities, and bolster
enivironimental monitoring. In addition to national regulatory assistance, the Bank Group is
currently involved in tive urban environmental projects in eight cities, which incorporate
policy, financing and organizational strategies for improved environmental regulation and
service delivery at the provincial and municipal levels. Another four ongoing investment
projects involving 12 cities include related issues such as municipal water supply and
sanitation needs. The initial round of projects focused on China's largest cities-Shanghai.
Beijing, and Tianjin-and those located primarily in the wealthier northeast and coastal
provinces. Environmental pressures remain very high in those areas and cooperation will
continue, as with the recently appraised Second Shanghai Sewerage Project and the
Shiandong Environmenit Project IIow being prepared. However, the proposed Hubei Urban
Environmental l'roject marks a shift in geographic focus, moving toward inland and poorer
areas. I lubei, a mieedium-income province, is located in central China, wvhile projects now
hbing prepared in thle very poor Yunnan and Guangxi provinces will further test the
t'easibility of policy and institutional solutions.
2.   3Ban;kwide municipal management sector work (Urban Service Delivery: Finding
the Right Incentives) emphasizes the need to properly assign functional responsibilities
and link revenue to expenditures.  In China's case, environmental services are
appropriately provided at the municipal level, where a high percentage of government
reventue is also captured. China: Urhan Environmental .Service Management concludes
that the major failing in environmental services has been dependence on municipal
budget transfers to fund services, while greater reliance on user charges would be
af'fordable, induce resource conservation, and create a more dependable income stream.
For that reason, increasing tariffs has been a major focal point of the policy dialogue in
this project. This responds, too, to a major finding in the OED review of Bank water and
sanitation projects (Water Supply and Sanitation Projects: The Bank Experience 1967-
1'089), wherein physical performance was generally good, but sustaining financial



-157-                       ANNEX 14
viability and overall sectoral development was less successful. Adequate sector revenues
have been so crucial to successful operation of these investments that the Bank now sees the
needed tariff structures as the major sign of municipal commitment and therefore asks for
initial adjustments to be made during project processing. The proposed project follows this
model.
3.    This project thus responds to Bank sector work and sector experience, and CAS
objectives, first through an improved regulatory environment, reduction of current pollution
loads, and protection of critical water and land resources and second through
commercialization of operating agencies, and improved tariff policy and operating
techniques. The Bank's strategy flows from and supports China's Environmental Action
Plan and Agenda 21, which provide the basis for joint Government and Bank action in the
environmental sector, especially in regard to the urban environment. Finally, the provincial
focus recognizes the effective decentralization of regulatory and investment authority in
China, and draws on local political power and commitment to induce wider use of the
solutions demonstrated under the project.
4.    The project supports provincial efforts to substantially reduce pollution within the
province, especially within densely populated urban areas, by providing new models for the
efficient organization and financing of urban environmental services and financing for
wastewater and solid waste handing and treatment, and for industrial pollution control.
Specifically, the project would support:
(a)   the more aggressive use of user fees to shift the burden of pollution
abatement to the polluter and induce waste minimization and new
organizational forms for wastewater providers, to allow improved
management tools and to help minimize the cost of abatement;
(b)   the provincial and municipal EPBs in their efforts to implement more
effective regulatory instruments;
(c)   expansion of sewerage services, hence a reduction in water pollution, within
the boundaries of Wuhan, Xiangfan, and Huangshi, thus protecting the
urban environment and the health of their people;
(d)   the first municipal solid waste landfills meeting national standards in the
cities of Xiangfan and Yichang, and major capacity expansion in Huangshi
and Wuhan, with consequent protection of local land and water resources
and flaring of landfill methane to reduce greenhouse gas emissions; and
(e)   a line of credit to allow abatement of important industrial point sources of
pollution affecting large urban populations and a pollution control revolving
fund to provide a model for such funds.



-158-                         ANNEX 14
B. ECONONlic ANALYSIS
5.    From  an economics perspective, project formulation-within the constraint of
developing an urban environment project-presented the major analytical challenge of
allocating investment support across a potentially wide array of cities and sectors. Ideally,
that allocation would maximize net present value, fully incorporating environmental
externalities, within the project area. Yet, current methodologies do not allow quick or
inexpensive, yet high confidence. estimates of the impact of interventions across such a
broad choice set. Instead, a different macro allocation criterion was used, based on the fact
that what was known with high confidence was that current policy and institutional
structures needed substantial change if sustainable environmental protection was to be
aclieved.  Policy problems included urbani environmental services priced too low to
discourage waste generation or provide the financing needed to build, maintain, and operate
services suclh as wastewater collection and treatment. Government bureaus providing the
services lacked the financial tools to develop effective tariff strategies or the operating
autonomny needed to encourage managerial iimovation. Gaps in environmental regulation
Vwcr: allso apparent. Therefore, choices about participating sectors and cities were made
vith a view toward maximizing the impact of policy aned inistitutional innovation within the
province. Specific investment choices within sectors and cities responded to municipal
government strategies to bring ambient environmental quality or service standards up to
national guidelines. For the latter, analyvsis sought the least cost option for meeting the
guidelines. The detailed calculations can be found in the project files. Highlights of the
analysis are given below.
C ity And Sector Choices
6.     I lubei Province has developed environmental improvement plans with annual, five
year, and ten year horizons. These plans include upgraded urban environmental services in
all of flubei's 30 cities and a focus on the 117 most highly polluting enterprises, most of
wvhich are within city administrative boundaries. In urban environmental services, the plans
higlhlight the relative lack of sewerage services within urbani areas-household and
industrial wastewater typically discharges to creeks, rivers, and lakes rather than to sewer
systems-and the virtual absence of sanitary landfills or other safe disposal for solid waste
and nightsoil.  Enterprise pollution abatement strategies locus on process change,
siometimes accompanied by physical relocation of the enterprise.
7.    In urban environmental service provision, cities were included in the project based
on the size of the population potentially benefiting from improved services and considering
their value as models to other provincial cities. After reviewing potential investments in
some nine provincial cities, the four largest cities were selected for further project
development. Each of those cities had already prepared environmental improvement plans
that outlined priority service improvements, both in terms of the type of service and areas of
the city needing service. With the project, each city established a council headed by a vice-
mayor, with members from the environmental protection, urban services, plamning, and
financial bureaus, to review existing plans and guide any decisions needed on scope or



- 159 -                       ANNEX 14
administrative arrangements. Work with Bank missions colnfirmed the choice to extend
sewerage systems and build or substantially expand sanitary landfills.
8.    The Hubei strategy for industrial pollution control builds on national and provincial
emissions control law and regulations. However, the current concentration-based emissions
controls can produce the perverse results that even if all enterprises comply, high ambient
pollution levels can result where receiving body volume is low compared to effluent
volumes, or, when receiving body volumes and other characteristics create very high
assimilative capacity compared to loads, enterprises will spend an excessive amount on
pollution control. In response, the province is moving to a mass-based permit system, the
implementation of which is an element of the Environmental Improvement Action Plan in
this project.
9.    For enterprises built before polluting effluents were regulated, cash flow alone
rarely meets the financing needs of process changes or end-of-pipe treatment that will
allow compliance with current effluent standards. Pollution abatement investments must
compete in China's plan-driven credit market with investments typically offering much
higher financial returns to sponsors. Where banks are able to make loans outside of state-
dictated  credit plans, they  seek  high  and  rapid  returli opportunities, a feature
uncharacteristic of pollution-abatement investments. Credit market constraints [described
much more fully in the China Department's 1995 Capital Markets Sector Study (No.
14501-CHA)] do leave a large number of abatement opportunities in economically and
financially viable enterprises that currently have a high pollution impact on large
populations.  Hubei Province therefore sought to use part of the loan to fund such
investments.  The Province agreed with the Bank on a selection process to identify
enterprise pollution abatement programs that would have a high positive impact on urban
populations and provide them access to a line-of-credit to finance the interventions. This
line-of-credit activity is discussed more fully below.
10.   The potential Bank loan for the municipal service and enterprise pollution control
activities was far below the absorptive capacity of the province. Either urban environmental
services or the industrial pollution control component could have absorbed the entire loan
earmnarked for the province bv the State Planning Commission.  Given that urban
environmental problems could be successfully addressed only by a combination of urban
servize and industrial pollution control measures, and that the high visibility of Bank-
financed operations was thought important to providing model approaches to outstanding
prob:ems, the HPG and Bank agreed that both types of activities should be incorporated
into :he loan. The allocation of the loan between the two types of activities, approximately
two ihirds to municipal services and one third to industrial pollution control, resulted from a
shared judgment about the balance that would produce maximum impact within the
province. Decisions about resource allocation within sectors and cities are detailed below:



-160 -                        ANNEX 14
Wastewater Collection and Treatment
11.   Wuhan. Currently, some 60 percent of the 1.8 million m3 average daily wastewater
flows within the city of Wuhan discharge to sewers. In Wuchang district, a wastewater
treatment plant with 50,000 m3 daily capacity was recently commissioned, but a lack of
operating funds idles the plant for several months each year. Unsewered wastewater
discharges to lakes, canals, and rivers within the municipality, all of which eventually
discharge to the Yangtze. The quality of these surface waters has deteriorated rapidly in
recent years, with that in the largest lake, East Lake (see Map 27250), now Class IV' in
many areas and experiencing heavy eutrophication, Moshui Lake well below Class V,
Nanhu Lake with BOD5 and cyanide over Class V standard and Nantaizi Lake plagued by
eutrophication. Most smaller canals and rivers fail to meet Class V, although the Fu river
does meet Class IV. Nonetheless, East Lake has been a major raw water source for
domestic use in Wuchang district, with installed capacity equal to 10 percent of all Wuhan
municipal supply, and its waters are used for irrigation, commercial and recreational
fishing, other recreation, and industrial supply. Nanhu Lake serves the same functions with
the exception of domestic potable water supply. Moshui has been lost to use except as a
waste sink.
12.   The Yangtze and Han Rivers split Wuhan into three large districts, all of which lack
adequate sewering and wastewater treatment. To facilitate the introduction of institutional
and tariff reforms throughout the city, the Wuhan municipal government sought to include
Bank-supported work in each of the three districts.  Investment priority, based on
anticipated impact on water quality and the associated population, was first diversion of
wastewater from East Lake in the Wuchang district; second capture of flows in west
Hankou district, with discharge to the Fu River; and third, diversion of wastewater from
Nantaizi Lake and Moshui Lake in Hanyang district, with preliminary treatment and
discharge to the Yangtze.
13.   Direct economic losses to East Lake pollution include those of water supply losses
(averaging an additional Y 20 million/year as the cost of replacing East Lake supply with
that from the Yangtze), additional health costs and lost recreational and development
potential. The latter two costs are more difficult to evaluate than additional water supply
costs, but Wuhan research institutes have attached estimated values of Y 21 million and
Y 12 million annually to these losses. At a 12 percent discount rate, that represents a net
present value of Y 401 million to the pollution damage, assuming that the worsening water
quality would not cause further real increases in costs. The cheapest remedy for these
losses would be to capture the wastewater flows and discharge them without treatment to
the Yangtze, with its higher assimilative capacity. Investment costs for this approach would
total about Y 300 million. However, such discharges to the Yangtze are no longer
The water quality classification system: Classes I and I: high quality suitable for drinking water
source; Class Ill: usable for drinking water after treatment; Class IV: boating, fishing, industrial water
supply; Class V: irrigation and industrial cooling.



-161-                       ANNEX14
permitted by the central govermment, in an attempt to preserve Yangtze quality at its current
Class II level. The alternative selected, of expanding existing tertiary and secondary sewers
on the western side of East Lake (see Map 27250) and expanding the existing wastewater
treatment plant at Shahu, would permit primary treatment of up to 230,000 rn3/day of
wastewater diverted from East Lake. An additional 150,000 m3/day of wastewater would
be picked up on the south side of East Lake for discharge to South Lake after primary
treatment. At an investment cost of approximately Y 640 million, these two interventions
would divert and treat nearly all sewage flowing into East Lake and lead to the following
estimated change in East Lake quality over time:
Table 1: EAST LAKE PHOSPHORUS CONCENTRATIONS
Time (years)             1            3             5               10
Total Phosphorus        60           32            26               24
(mg/m 3)
14.   This would, for this indicator, improve water quality from Class IV to Class II.
These estimates assume the inflow of Yangtze River water needed to maintain the water
balance of East Lake, works for which would be included under the proposed project. Even
with the wastewater flow abated, total phosphorus levels remain high because of
phosphorus release from lake sediments. This problem is even more severe in other lakes
and marks the difficulty of finding short-run solutions to water quality problems. The
option of dredging the lake to remove polluted sediments was rejected as less effective than
further extensions of the sewerage network.
15.   An interceptor sewer would pick up 70 percent of sewage currently discharging to
Moshui Lake, with high quality water flows from the Han River providing water balance.
As noted above, Moshui serves solely as a waste sink at this point and no estimate has been
made of foregone services compared to a lake in this location with usable water quality.
Even with the planned diversion, given the very high pollutant loads in Moshui Lake
sediments, water quality recovery is expected to take more than the 10 years needed by East
Lake. Nonetheless, that recovery can be expected to bring the lake within Class V quality
within a decade. The least cost approach relies on an open settling basin, rather than
conventional treatment, to provide some solids removal prior to discharge to the Yangtze.
16.   The densely populated West Hankou area currently discharges wastewater through
open channels carrying combined stormwater and wastewater flows that eventually enter
the Fu River. That river currently rates as Class IV or V, depending on measurement site.
The existing open channels lack sufficient capacity for the growing wastewater volume, and
overflow into streets, fish ponds and agricultural land is common. The city plan is to
upgrade collection, providing the needed capacity to avoid overflows and, where possible,
fully enclosing the sewers. Discharge would continue to the Fu River, after preliminary
treatment. No quality improvement in that river would be possible until the addition of
further treatment, which is contemplated after the year 2000. The least cost solution sought



-162-                        ANNEX 14
to maximize capture of flows currently discharging to open channels within the overall
budget constraint on this component.
17.   Xiangfan. Currently, Xiangfan city wastewater flows discharge through tertiary
and some secondary sewers to the nearest river or, in the Xiangcheng district, the city moat.
As a result, those surface waters are highly polluted, with the smaller rivers such as the
Qilihe and Xiaoqinghe failing to reach Class V water quality, the lowest permissible for any
human use. Nonetheless, the water is used for irrigation, some is extracted for industrial
use, and human contact continues through uses such as fishing. A large number of
discharges go directly to the Han River, generally downstream of the municipal and
industrial raw water irntakes. Despite the high assimilative capacity of the Han, that river
falls from the Class I to Class II range above the city to Class III or IV below the city. The
Han stands to lose a third of its flow to the South-North water transfer project over the next
decade, and as it serves as a key raw water source down to Wuhan, the quality of its water is
a particular concern for the province. As the main upstream city, Xiangfan is under
pressure to minimize polluting inputs to the river.
18.   Xiangfan has designed a collection and treatment system for its wastewaters, but has
lacked adequate financing to install the system. After a review of the Xiangfan design and
assessment of costs and counterpart funding ability, it was clear that even with Bank
support the entire system could not be constructed within the time frame of this project.
Based on Xiangfan's counterpart funding ability, and sizing for year 2010 flows, the
physical scope of the project was determined by working upstream from the proposed
treatment plant site. Treatment was set at the preliminary level after an assimilative
capacity study showed that the 6.9 m3/sec municipal flows could continue to be discharged
directly to the Han with no net deterioration of Han quality. The new outfall will have
better dispersion characteristics than the current riverbank discharges. In anticipation of
later need, the preliminary treatment works are being designed and sufficient land is being
set aside for the addition of primary and secondary treatment as soon as that is feasible.
Because serving either the Xiangcheng or Fancheng districts required major river crossings,
only one could be supported under the project. The Fancheng crossing was substantially
less costly and the district has the larger wastewater flows, so the lowest cost per cubic
meter intercepted could be realized there. Conveyors of appropriate size were extended up
through the Fancheng catchment until funds ran out. This permitted most discharges to the
Xiaoqinghe and Qilihe, the largest rivers within Fancheng district, to be picked up. The
quality of those rivers should rise from below grade to approximately Class 111. It also
permitted interception of nearly all Fancheng neighborhood discharges to the Han, but here
the pollution impact was simply displaced downstream. Design and construction work on
further extensions of the system will be financed if the first phase realizes significant
savings compared to estimated construction costs.
19.   Huangshi. Huangshi is built back from the Yangtze and around Cihu Lake (see
Map 27175). The bulk of the urban wastewater discharges into Cihu, which itself
discharges through a pump station to the Yangtze. Cihu, which serves fishing, swimming
and amenity needs, has deteriorated from Class III to less than Class V on its southern side



-163 -                       ANNEX 14
over the past decade. Very rapid industrial and residential growth has accelerated nutrient
build up in the lake, which now experiences eutrophication that will soon become extremely
serious.

Total Phosphorus     Chlorophyll     Transparency  1

With project    2000               0.11                12.3              1.7
20.   The Huangshi urban development master plan calls for interceptor sewers that
would parallel the lake shore on both the north and south sides, picking up all wastewater
flows currently entering the lake. Counterpart funding and loan size constraints allow only
the first phase of this work to be undertaken in the next five years. The least cost strategy
was based on picking up the largest wastewater flows with the available funds, forgoing any
but preliminary treatment before discharge to the Yangtze. The engineering costing details
of the options are available in the project files. Because of similar composition of the
physical and labor inputs to the alternative investments, the engineering prices serve as an
adequate guide to comparative economic costs. The analysis showed that with the heavier
concentration of residential and industrial flows on the south side of the lake, the unit cost
of picking up flows was lower there. As this was also the most seriously polluted section of
the lake, the choice was made to build the south trunk sewer first. This sewer will serve
approximately 120,000 people. The south trunk sewer work alone is estimated to bring
Cihu Lake back to Class III water quality, eliminating the risk to current uses.
21.   Sewered flows will discharge to the Yangtze. The Yangtze assimilative capacity is
sufficiently high in relation to Huangshi flows (2.2 m3/sec for Huangshi vs. 23,800 m3/sec
for the Yangtze) that treatment is not needed at this stage. Therefore, as in Xiangfan, a
preliminary treatment plant will be built at a site large enough for, and with preliminarv
treatment works sized for, later expansion to primary and then secondary treatment.
Wastes Disposal
22.   Of the four project cities, only Wuhan and Huangshi have sanitary landfills or other
means of preventing solid waste from becoming a long term environmental threat. The
Wuhan site handles less than 25 percent of the city's municipal solid waste, and the
Huangshi site takes about one third of that city's municipal solid waste. The remaining
wastes go to either municipal dumps that, while well managed, lack any provision for
leachate or gas control, or to informal dumps that lack the management of the municipal
dumps. The other cities rely on a combination of municipal and informal dumps. Based on
international experience, dumps without leachate or gas control pose a long run threat to the
aquifers that run under them, to watercourses that receive leachate from them, and to



-164 -                         ANNEX 14
surrounding land. If landfill gasses are not collected and flared, they contribute substantial
amiounits of methane to the atmosphere. For these reasons, China has joined many other
countries in stipulating minimum standards for leachate and gas control in landfilling of
municipal solid waste.
23 *.   Potential solid waste disposal strategies include recycling, composting, incineration,
and landfilling. Recycling plays an active role in each of the cities, capturing most of the
5 percent of the municipal waste stream that can be profitably recycled. The industry is
highly competitive, withl little scope for additional useful municipal intervention.
Composting, to recycle organic materials, has been tested with scant success in Wuhan and
other Chinese cities. Costs have been high and markets have not supported product sales.
A major problem for composting and incineration is that over half of the waste stream is
inorganic, with coal ash the dominant inorganic component. Analysis in other Chinese
cities with similar waste streams has shown incineration to be substantially more costly than
landfill. Based on that work, each of the four cities hias elected to use landfilling to handle
their municipal solid wastes.
24.    Eaclh of the project cities faces the need to build new sites, as current sites are
approaching capacity. Cities have accepted that new solid waste disposal sites must comply
with national standards, so have made no attempt to undertake the very difficult comparison
of the cost of adding leachate and gas collection with the damage that would ensue from
their absence. Damage estimates would depend on leachate diffusion through aquifers,
systematic sampling of local water bodies to identify leachate impacts, then estimation of
economic uses of those waters over time and the cost of forgone use or pollution
amelioration. Gas emission volumes and dispersion would be easier to estimate, but
damage valuation would be very uncertain. Given the long run risks inherent in unprotected
landfills, the Bank accepted the municipal judgment on the need to provide leachate and gas
collection and treatment without additional impact evaluation.
25.    Once the decision was made on landfill standards, each city faced the need to
identify the least cost site and design. Wuhan, Xiangfan, Yichang, and Huangslhi each
considered a number of alternative locations, with one of the main decision criteria being to
minimize the joint cost of transport, land acquisition and site development. As land
acquisition cost correlates very strongly with resettlement impact, this criteria also
minimizes resettlement. Yichang identified two potential sites with similar haul distances
and resettlement impact. The site chosen was preferred because the other site was in a new
industrial development zone and thought to be an inappropriate use of land in such a zone.
Huangshi narrowed its choices to three sites, with the Xisai site preferred for easy access
and minimum impact on surrounding populations. Wuhan had an existing site with suitable
land available for expansion. To take advantage of existing infrastructure, no other sites
were considered. Detailed assessment of the choices by each city is described in the project
files. Xiangfan has two possible sites that appear to have similar unit costs. Detailed
analysis has been completed on one site and continues on the other.



-165-                         ANNEX 14
Industrial Pollution Control
26.   The industrial pollution control component has two subcomponents, one a line of
credit for major investments and the other a pollution control fund targeting small
investments and small-scale enterprises. Eligibility for the line of credit was restricted in
the first instance to Hubei's 117 most polluting industrial enterprises, which are responsible
for over 60 percent of industrial pollution in Hubei. The toxicity of enterprises' pollutants
and the population affected by the pollution were evaluated. The higlhest-imiipact firms were
then subject to a simple economic screening that eliminated (a) all enterprises which had a
negative net worth and were losing money, and (b) any enterprise whose abatement strategy
depended on production of a commodity in which the enterprise had no prior experience.
With priority based on their environmental impact, remaining firms were then eligible for
more thorough technical, financial, and economic appraisal. The funds available for
subloans would finance pollution control programs for approximately six firms, if current
investment plans remain unchanged after appraisal. One appraisal of Ezhou Iron & Steel
(Egang) was successfully undertaken during overall project appraisal, to test the quality of
local appraisal efforts.  The remainder will be done in the early months of project
effectiveness.
27.   Technical appraisal will focus on feasibility and efficiency:  is operation of the
proposed investment within the technical capabilities of the firm, and will it result in
production clean enough to meet national and local discharge standards while yielding a
significant improvement in ambient air or water quality for a significant population. The
economic appraisal will look at whether the technical choice was the most economically
efficient among technical alternatives and whether the input and output market prospects
support the sustainability of the financial appraisal results. The financial appraisal will
focus on the company as a whole, rather than on the particular technology. This is because
the most effective intervention may be end-of-pipe treatment of an effluent stream, with no
financial return other than cessation of effluent fees.
28.   The successful appraisal covered two investments at the enterprise ranked as most
environmentally damaging among viable firms. Details of the appraisal may be found in
the Project Files. The key appraisal finding showed the firm to be financially healthy and
economically viable. The firm benefited from progressive price liberalization after 1992,
and in 1994 had net profits of Y 220 million on sales of Y 1,520 million and low debt
compared to the industry average. The latter finding was particularly important given the
rapid integration of China's steel industry into the international market economy. In
brief, the first Egang investment will replace a 40-year old electric arc furnace that is
responsible for 23 percent of the TSP concentrations within an urban area containing some
200,000 people. The proposed investment is in a new furnace, incorporating up-to-date air
pollution control, which will produce virtually no particulates or NO, and will improve air
quality for affected people from Class III to Class IL. The new furnace will have higher
capacity than the old one and will also use less electricity per ton of steel produced. TFlc
investment will yield an estimated 12 percent financial and 23 percent economic rate ot
return on the investment, where the economic rate of return incorporates none of die air



-166-                           ANNEX 14
quality gains. Most of the gap between the financial and economic rates of return is due to
the fact that Egang steel is priced 20 to 30 percent below CIF imported prices for equivalent
products. That gap leaves substantial room to absorb price pressures should the world steel
market soften. Table 3 summarizes the economic return calculation.
Table 3: ECONOMIC RETURNS
(Steel production is in thousand tons/year. Costs and revenue are in million yuan)
1995   1996   1997   1998    1999    2000      2001-2014
Incremental Steel Production  0      0       0      0       0      50          50
Incremental Revenue           0      0       0      0      18     224         230
Incremnental Production Cost  0      0       0      0    -32       65          55
Investment Cost              17   227    151       53
Net                         -17  -227   -151    -53        50     159         175
Economic Rate of Return = 23%
Note: The new furnace would begin production in 1999, producing 150,000 tons that year and reaching
full capacity of 300,000 tons by 2001. The with-project steel is higher quality and thus commands a
higher price.
29.    The analysis also looked at the sensitivity of the economic rate of returns to
various changes. The four factors judged to have the greatest impact on the economic
rate of return were changes in the economic price of steel, an increase in input costs
relative to sales prices, higher-than-anticipated capital costs, and lower-than-anticipated
efficiency levels. Egang steel prices are already over 20 percent below border prices for
the product and a further 10 percent price drop would drop the ERR by 3 percent. An
increase in input costs would have relatively little impact on the ERR because it would
have a nearly equal impact on the without-project scenario, where steel production
continues with the current furnace-a 10 percent cost increase would drop the ERR by
only I percent. The ERR is also relatively insensitive to capital cost increases, with a
2 percent drop for a 10 percent capital cost increase. A further factor considered was a
"learning curve" in using the new fumace, whereby operating efficiency would be below
the norm for, say, the first three years of operation. Given the large staff of engineers and
operators experienced with electric-arc furnaces, three years is seen as an outer bound for
those efficiency  losses.   Of these various factors, the risk of "learning  curve"
inefficiencies and steel price decreases are judged most likely (although vis-a-vis the
international market, upside price potential would appear to outweigh downside). To
drop the ERR to the test level of 12 percent, a combination of factors including a
20 percent drop in sales price (to over 40 percent below international market levels), a
I0 percent increase in capital costs, and a 10 percent "learning curve" drop in efficiency
would have to occur. This or similar adverse combinations are judged highly unlikely
given Egang's operating experience and recent market history in the key input and output
markets.



-167-                        ANNEX 14
30.   The high and robust economic returns to the proposed air pollution control
investment are an example of a "win-win" solution to an environmental problem.
However, Egang also includes an example of damaging wastewater discharges that are
difficult to value, and the main available intervention increases operating costs with no
corresponding increase in a marketable product. Egang discharges some 17 tons a year of
phenol, 11.8 tons of cyanide, and 462 tons of oil, as well as substantial solids and COD
into local watercourses and then the Yangtze.  The phenol and cyanide are over
90 percent of all such discharges in Ezhou city and the oil 63 percent, with the result that
the major urban waterways fall to category IV for phenol and oil, and II for cyanide.
31.   To reduce these discharges, Egang will substantially revamp their wastewater
handling system. All discharges currently entering the Yanglan Lake or Xingang River
will be intercepted, treated, and reused, ending Egang's contribution to their pollution.
Yanglan Lake is fished and used as an industrial water supply source. Xingang stream
provides potable water for villagers and a source of industrial water supply.  No
epidemiological work has been done on the impact of Egang discharges through fish
consumption or domestic water supply, so damage estimates were not possible. The most
serious discharges, from the coking plant, will continue to discharge directly to the
Yangtze. However, a new coking wastewater treatment plant financed by the project will
radically reduce pollutants in that discharge, with, for example, phenol falling from 1.53
tons/year to 0.45 tons/year and oil from 32.4 tons/year to 6.5 tons/year. The net effect of
the investment will be to bring the Xingang River and Yuanlan Lake to Class II in tllese
pollutants, allowing safe use of the water. The cost is high-an estimated Y 130 million
or $13 million.
32.   Based on the industrial pollution control ranking system, investments in Egang are
anticipated to have the most substantial positive environmental impacts. The remaining
top-ranked firms on the short list are two cement plants, wastewater and waste gas treatment
at two chemical plants and wastewater treatment at a pharmaceutical plant.
33.   The environmental pollution control fund remains to be established, so cannot be
assessed in the same way the line-of-credit can be. The goal of the fund differs from the
line of credit, in that it strives to serve as a model for the operation of the municipal and
county run pollution control funds capitalized through pollution levy fees. These are
revolving funds, but to date have lacked effective management, targeting, and credit
policies. Hence, while the appraisal goals are similar to the line of credit, the target firms
and pollution problems are much smaller scale. The fund will seek to minimize
management costs, while effectively recycling loans. The fund will bear credit risk, for,
like the line of credit, banks will play a solely agent role. Again, like the line of credit,
loans will be made at a rate at least 150 basis points above the Bank variable rate, the
margin to be determined by operating costs and credit risk assessment. However, the fund
will not have the direct power of the finance bureau to encourage timely loan servicing by
enterprises. Also, it will face a history of disbursement of pollution levy fees as grants. The
fund will respond to these risks in two ways. First, by careful financial analysis and



-168-                       ANNEX 14
conservative lending and second by concentrating on smaller loans with relatively short
repayment periods.
C. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT
34.   The project would contribute significantly to improving Hubei's environment, in
ways detailed in the body of the SAR and the annexes. Potential adverse impacts from the
sanitary landfill and industrial pollution control components require an "A" category in
terms of the World Bank's Operational Directive on Environmental Assessment (EA).
Accordingly, a full EA has been completed, reviewed by the Bank and approved by NEPA.
The full report can be found in the project files. Annex 8 details the EA findings and
reflects the findings of the appraisal mission. The resettlement impact of the project will be
managed through a resettlement action plan that can be found in the project files and is
summarized in Annex 7.
D. AFFORDABILITY AND POVERTY ASSESSMENT
35.   The affordability issue arises only with the wastewater components of the proposed
project. The industrial pollution interventions will proceed only if the enterprise can
demonstrate that it will remain financially sound after the investment, and solid waste
charges will rise only for industrial and commercial users of the new landfills.
Affordability of the new wastewater systems was tested against current consumption
pattems and average income of the poorest 10 percent of the urban population in the three
cities. The proposed first year tariff would consume an average of 0.25 percent of the
income of the poorest 10 percent, based on 80 percent of water used. Indeed, because the
poor are less likely than other households to have water piped to the house (using
standpipes instead, for which no fees are currently charged), even the 0.25 percent figure
represents an upper bound.
36.   The affordability of the higher tariffs anticipated in later years will clearly depend
on changes in real income among the urban poor over time. In real terms, the tariffs will
increase to as much as twice the anticipated 1996 rate. With no change in real income and
no consumption effect, the average wastewater bill would increase to 0.50 percent of
average income for the poorest 10 percent of households. However, over the past decade,
the real incomes of the urban poor have risen in line with overall income increases. If that
pattem, and overall real personal income growth, continues near current rates, wastewater
tariffs as a percentage of personal income would remain virtually unchanged from the first
year level. In absolute terms, the increase would be fully offset by first year income gains.
E. PROJECT RisKs
37.   The proposed project faces or mitigates a variety of risks. Those risks are here
grouped under three general headings: implementation and operational risks, regulatory
risk, and risks to long term sustainability. Each type of risk will be discussed in turn.



-169-                       ANNEX 14
Implementation and Operation Risks
38.   These risks include a variety of institutional, physical, and financial factors that
could interfere with the timely construction or operation of any of the project elements, or
with the implementation of the institutional innovations. Specifically, these include:
Municipal Components
39.   Procurement Risks: Lack of familiarity with Bank procurement procedures and
policies may lead to delayed procurement. Experience in a wide array of Bank projects has
shown this risk to be substantial among new borrowers. HUEPO's use of an experienced
Chinese tendering company (already engaged) will help mitigate the risk in preparing
commercial documents. The plan to use international consultants experienced with Bankl
financed projects to provide advisory services on preparation of technical documents.
HUEPO will sign a contract for the latter services by negotiations and the consultant team
will be in place well before effectiveness.
40.   Financial Risks: These risks are of two sorts, those of counterpart funding and
operational funding. Counterpart funding risks derive from the fact that the Bank loan
covers only part of the construction costs. The wastewater agencies are financing a
relatively small proportion of the investment out of current income, and that income
depends on implementation of the new tariffs (discussed further under policy risk). Given
the inelastic demand for water in the three cities (on consumption of which the wastewater
charges are based), the conservative billing assumptions used, and the fact that the initial
tariff increase will be implemented before project effectiveness, the risk of inadequate
wastewater company revenues is deemed to be low. The bulk of the counterpart funds are
allocated from the municipal budget. Indeed, to minimize risks in this area, municipal
budgets were analyzed under the assumption that all counterpart funding would come from
that source.  Those budgets have been analyzed under growth assumptions that are
conservative compared to recent experience. Even then, counterpart funding requirements
would amount to less than 3 percent of on-budget revenues and no more than 40 percent of
recent on-budget capital investment allocations in all but Xiangfan. Given the importance
of off-budget revenues to cities (up to half of total revenue), both of those indicators suggest
a higher exposure than actually exists.
41.   Xiangfan's investment program would demand up to 4 percent of annual total on-
budget expenditures and would represent, in the peak year, a doubling of the historical on-
budget capital expenditures. Again, given the growing importance of off-budget income
and investment, these figures overstate project exposure in Xiangfan. As with all the
project cities, Xiangfan has identified and committed to specific funding sources for this
project. Furthermore, the Bank sought independent analysis from the provincial finance
bureau of the availability of and risks to the stated funding sources. Based on the Bank and
provincial finance bureau assessments, the Xiangfan investment program was pared down
to its current level, as representing a feasible investment under the conservative growth
assumptions.  Nonetheless, a substantial slowdown in municipal revenue growth, which



-170 -                        ANNEX 14
would likely accompany any major downturn in the national economic cycle, would force
the city to cut back on ongoing investments.  Under similar circumstances, local
governments in other Bank-supported projects in China have sometimes reduced their
annual counterpart funding, lengthening the construction period. In Xiangfan's case,
counterpart funding risks are minimized during the first year of implementation by the fact
that the appraisal of the Xiangfan solid waste component has been delayed (for technical
reasons unrelated to financing). This will allow overall progress in the first year of project
implementation to be evaluated before committing to the additional landfill investment.
42.   In terms of operational funding, Hubei province and the cities have agreed to a
financial covenant that will, for wastewater operations, generate sufficient revenues
through operations to meet anticipated operational, depreciation, and loan repayment
needs.  Initial tariff adjustments prior to project effectiveness, in addition, allow a
contribution to capital. Solid waste operations will continue to rely on direct government
allocations for a part of their operating costs, given the difficulty of billing households on
a waste volume basis (per capita or per household taxes substitute for this). However, the
ability fully to cost and charge for the important commercial and industrial services will
increase revenues to the environmental sanitation bureaus, reducing dependence on the
city and the risk of inadequate operating funds.
43.   Construction Risk:  The proposed investments differ little in construction
demands from other work already successfully undertaken in the cities themselves, or
elsewhere in the province. The one possible exception to this would come if the landfill
in Yichang is deemed to need an artificial liner. Such liners have been rarely used in
China and need particular care during installation and operation. To minimize this risk,
the installation contract will require firms to demonstrate successful experience in the
field.
44.   Operational Risk: This risk is deemed to be very small due to the fact that all of
the proposed technologies are in successful operation either in the cities themselves, or in
other Chinese cities or plants with staff of equivalent background and training. The
technical assistance planned under the project would further mitigate this risk by providing
operational training on all newly constructed municipal facilities.
45.   The shift to new organizational forms for wastewater management is critical to
project success.  All cities are well along in the process, with Xiangfan having
successfully completed the transformation to an enterprise. In Huangshi, assets have been
revalued and that work is being reviewed by the State Asset Bureau, the last step before
formal transfer. Wuhan has begun revaluing assets and expects to undertake the transfer by
the end of 1995, well before planned effectiveness. The groundwork is thus laid for
introduction of the new accounting and management information systems needed to
efficiently guide the work of these enterprises. In the long run, efficient operation will
also depend on cities honoring the autonomy of the enterprises in personnel and other
management decisions. This element of risk cannot be divorced from larger efforts
within the economy to reforrn government relations with the state owned enterprise



-171-                        ANNEX 14
sector. These issues have recently received thorough analysis in State Owned Enterprise
Reform (currently in yellow cover). The solid waste sector presents a rather different
challenge, as this will continue in bureau form. Here, the challenge is to introduce the
new accounting tools. The risk to this is minimized by the advantage to the ESB of being
able to identify costs and thus justify the tariffs agreed to by the cities.
Industrial pollution control component
46.   Two main types of risk to the industrial pollution control component will be
addressed here. These are credit risk and the risk that the pollution abatement goals are
not met.
47.   Credit risk under the project is borne by the provincial government. Under the
line of credit, the agent bank, for a fee, undertakes project appraisal and handles fund
administration on behalf of the provincial government. An essential element of the
project appraisal, which is subject to review and confirmation by the provincial finance
bureau, is analysis of the firm's financial strength and the risk it faces in its input and
output markets. The firm must meet a 1.4 debt service coverage requirement over the
project life, providing that margin of safety to the government exposure. Representatives
of the provincial finance bureau have stated that their subjective estimate of the credit risk
in the project approaches zero. They argue that within the Chinese system as currently
structured, the finance bureau has sufficient discretionary power over the state owned
enterprises seeking access to the line of credit that they have primacy over all other
creditors. Nonetheless, the loans are being made at no less than 150 basis points over the
Bank variable lending rate, in US dollars, with the enterprise bearing the foreign
exchange risk. With fees to the agent Bank amounting to no more than 50 basis points,
the provincial government will capture at least 100 basis points to cover their costs and
risks. In addition, they will enjoy the use of loan and interest payments in the interval
between collection from the enterprise and repayment to the Ministry of Finance.
48.   Given the well-known failure of many enterprises to operate their pollution
abatement facilities, the analysis addressed the risk that pollution abatement goals would
not be met. Based on the short-listed project designs, this risk is deemed to be very
small. The reason is that, although not a requirement for access to the line-of-credit, the
bulk of the proposed investments are in process changes. The pollution abatement in
these investments derives not from add-on equipment, but from the intrinsic properties of
the production process compared to the original. Thus, the proposed electric arc furnace
for Egang does not have a "dirty" operating mode that could be used to save power or
other inputs. Nor do the proposed kilns in the cement plants. An exception to this pattern
can be found in the wastewater treatment plans for Egang. Here, one part of the strategy
involves treating and reusing the cleanest part of the wastewater stream, and the reuse
demand will help ensure adequate treatment. But the coke oven wastewater will, after
treatment, discharge as before to the Yangtze. If the treatment plant were to shut down,
enterprise operation would be cheaper but not otherwise affected. The only way to
mitigate this risk is aggressive monitoring and application of heavy fines for violations.



-172 -                        ANNEX 14
The local EPB does not have a history of such an approach, but has indicated that because
the EPB system helped Egang gain access to the line of credit, Egang now has a moral
obligation to operate the equipment, an obligation that did not previously exist.
Moreover, the EPB system is more generally stepping up its pollution levy fee collections
and pollution licensing system. so the new equipment will come on line in an era of
increased attention to compliance.
Policy/Regulatory Risk
49.   Major policy risks include: (a) possible resistance to timely implementation of the
new tarif'f structure and (b) that the sustained investment program needed for full wastes
control stretches well beyond the closing date of the proposed project and must be
continued to hold the water, solid waste and air pollution levels within efficient limits.
Thlese risks are all characterized by the importance of positive government action at either
the provincial or municipal level. The related institutional innovation risk was analyzed
earlier.
50.   Of the above risks, failure to implement agreed tariffs would seriously impair the
performance incentives of the utilities and their ability to finance ongoing investment
programs. To mitigate this risk, a tariff increase before negotiation is being required and
tariffs will be a supervision focal point. The latter will be aided by the fact that service
providers are required to present annual accounts showing whether existing tariffs have met
their financial needs and to project tariff levels needed in the following year.
51.   To encourage continued implementation of the broader environmental services
investment program, the proposed project includes funds for further project preparation and
introduces a tariff strategy that will sustain future investment. At least as important to
minimizing risk in the long run program is increasing citizen attention to and demand for
environmental improvements. That trend is being spurred by a special committee of the
National People's Congress. While citizen awareness is not supported directly by the
project, the spread of the investments is designed to provide solid evidence of the gains
from pollution control and thus gamer support for further action.
52.   Significant regulatory risks arise because the pollution control results anticipated
for the surface waters depend not only on sewerage technologies successfully constructed
and operated by the cities. Many important pollutants must be controlled at source by the
industries generating them.  A failure to do so could threaten either the sewerage
int'rastructure or the quality of the various lakes and rivers, depending on enterprise
discharge points. The principal means of mitigating this risk is a strong regulatory
regime. The importance of this work was recognized during preparation of the project
and is addressed under the environmental action plan (Annex I1).
Risks to Long-Term Sustainability
53.   The major risks to long term sustainability are financial and regulatory, and have
been discussed above. Additional risks arise from outside of the cities themselves and



-173 -                       ANNEX 14
center on polluting inputs to the various water bodies from areas beyond municipal
control. These additional pollutants could overwhelm any benefit that would otherwise
be realized by the planned investments. In the case of the network of waterways within
the cities, this risk is not great. Most of their watersheds are small, pollutants are of local
origin, and their control will be a function of the proper operation of the project.
However, the Han and Yangtze suffer from nonpoint source pollutants, particularly
agricultural, and carry substantial nutrient loads from upstream agriculture as well as
industrial pollution discharges. Satisfactory abatement of those polluting sources will
depend on the actions of a large number of political jurisdictions, most of which would
suffer no immediate harm from continued discharges to these rivers. The growing
appreciation within China of the importance of water pollution abatement offers the best
hope of inducing compliance with the current industrial regulations.  A  similar
appreciation of the risks from agriculture has yet to appear, and must become an element
of river basin quality control.
54.   Even if loads from other sources remain as predicted, minimizing any impact
from growing municipal loads will require a high degree of municipal treatment in
Xiangfan particularly.  There, the Han river faces the prospect of substantial flow
reductions if the South-North Water Transfer project proceeds. Even before those flows
fall, the environmental assessment for this project highlighted the importance of the Han
as a drinking water source and that absent early introduction of secondary treatment to
Xiangfan flows, the Han would fall below the Class II status that makes it a desirable raw
water source for municipal use.
